"There are many imitations," I said, and let the words settle, "but as you saw, I came to you in my smaller form, and led you here. You should know the truth."


The spider. Ground it in the spider, in the thing she had actually witnessed with her own eyes before any question of wizards or possession had entered her mind. A deft redirect — I was pleased with it — anchoring the claim in observable fact rather than asking her to simply take my word for a goddess.


And then the floor dropped out a second time.


"Oh, dear goodness," Asha breathed, and I watched her face change again — not into belief, not into suspicion, but into a third thing entirely, "have you two been infected by the spores, possessed by the crazed magic of Zuggtmoy? convinced of the bride's power? Is this another trick of Elvara's?"


She was not calling me a liar. She was asking whether I was a victim. Whether the lights and the spider and the quiet reverence were not deception or divinity but *infection* — the particular madness of a fungal god's influence playing tricks on a compromised mind.


I stood in the dark of the Underdark with a gelatinous cube behind me failing to be a rock, and I considered the specific difficulty of selling a lie to someone whose paranoia keeps generating better explanations than the truth.


## Zalthir — The Deception of Asha Vandry
There is a particular kind of silence that drow society uses as a weapon. Not the comfortable silence of empty stone corridors, not the productive silence of a hunter waiting for movement — the silence of deliberate erasure. Of being present in a room and being treated as furniture. I have spent enough time among my own kind to recognise it immediately. It never gets easier to absorb.


Asha Vandry noticed me the moment Daz beckoned and I stepped out of the shadow where I had been standing. I could see the calculation cross her face — male, surface-trained, drow enough to identify but not drow enough to acknowledge. She filed me away and returned her eyes to Daz. Not a glance afterward. Not a word in my direction for the duration of the conversation. Every question, every demand, every withering assessment delivered squarely to Daz, as though I were a piece of equipment he had brought along. A weapon he'd sheathed. A pack he'd set down.


I have noticed this before. I will notice it again. I noted it, set it aside, and watched.


Daz had already threaded us this far into the deception, which was impressive. Asha's opening had been a pointed thing — *"Have you two been infected by the spores, possessed by the crazed magic of Zuggtmoy? Convinced of the bride's power? Is this another trick of Elvara's?"* — the kind of question designed to see what shape your panic takes. Daz didn't panic. He gave her something more interesting: doubt worn as sincerity.


"I really do believe that I have," he said. "Perhaps there are some spores within me, but I… it feels… I feel it. And by the way, I've convinced others to join me."


The pause before *it feels* was good. The hesitation wasn't a crack — it was a texture. It made the conviction sound lived-in. I would have let her sit with that pause a moment longer, but Daz had his own tempo and it was working.


Then Glabbagool nearly ended everything.


The ooze had, apparently, opinions about the negotiation. It began to form the shape of an utterance — which is not something you want from what is supposed to be an inanimate ooze familiar — and Daz caught it with the ease of someone who has managed Glabbagool before.


"Glabbagool, I'm taking care of this, it's fine."


An ordinary sentence. The kind of thing you say to a dog that has started moving toward the table. Asha processed it as — I assume — some mild eccentricity of a spore-addled convert. The cube subsided. I did not visibly react. Somewhere behind my eyes, I filed it away in the archive of things I have witnessed that I will never be able to explain.


Asha began to talk about Ilvara, and the temperature of the room shifted. This was not careful diplomacy anymore. This was something older and sharper.


"Ilvara, who was once a high priestess of Lolth, has become seduced by this abomination, this idea that Lolth wishes to be married. There is something foul and stinking about her, and then all of those allies she seems to covet have turned into fungal creatures that are mindless. We need to kill her. We need to butcher her! We need to send her to an unearthly grave! I fear that her disease will spread."


Hatred and theology, wound together so tightly you couldn't separate one from the other. Asha's conviction about Lolth's will was absolute and cold, and the fury underneath it was not cold at all. She wanted Ilvara dead with a fervor that had been marinating in this cavern for gods know how long. I believed every word of it. The useful part wasn't the emotion — it was the intelligence hidden inside it. Ilvara's spores. A mushroom artifact. The specific vectors of infection. Asha had been studying this the way I study a tunnel entrance, and she had handed us the map without realizing she'd done it.


When Daz asked what they'd get for solving her problem, I watched Asha's posture tighten.


"The gratitude of Lolth," she said.


"I already feel the gratitude of Lolth," Daz said. "I have that."


I did not smile. But I noted it.


There is a specific kind of bold that is indistinguishable from foolishness until it works, and then it looks like mastery. Daz was operating in that register. Asha's response was to question the nature of his conviction — *"That is not the kind of conviction a follower of Lolth shows"* — and Daz didn't flinch.


"It's the kind of conviction this follower of Lolth shows."


She could have decided to kill us all at that moment. That was a real possibility. She didn't. Which told me something about how desperate she actually was.


That desperation became explicit when Daz pushed on the obvious question.


"How do you propose to kill her if you're not gonna get within 20 feet of her?"


A beat. Then: "I was not going to kill her. That is what you… where you come in."


The honesty had an almost refreshing quality. She needed us because she couldn't do this alone. Whatever power Asha held, she was not willing to test it against Ilvara's spores at close range, and she had been sitting in this cavern, waiting for a solution to walk through the door. We were, apparently, the solution.


She made this clear in her own way: "I thought you had strong allies, who had a tactical awareness and understanding how to use their magic for destructive purposes."


She was still talking to Daz. She was gesturing vaguely in the direction of the rest of us — which I presume included me, standing here, a person — but the words went to Daz. I considered pointing out that I was standing directly in front of her. I decided that would not advance anything useful.


Daz handled it smoothly, explaining that none of us were specialists in fungal corruption, and that asking before blundering in was the sensible approach — not ignorance, caution. Asha considered this.


"Well put, well put," she said. "This was just a test." A pause. "Don't touch me."


That last line. The flinch in it. Whatever confidence she projected, whatever command she assumed over the conversation, Asha Vandry was afraid of contamination in a very specific and personal way. She had been in proximity to Zuggtmoy's influence long enough to make her paranoid in a direction that felt earned.


We withdrew to confer. The terms were clear enough: Ilvara dies, the mushroom artifact is destroyed, the infection does not leave this cavern. Asha had confirmed the critical detail — the Heart Fungus itself wasn't the vector. The spores were Ilvara's. The artifact was the other risk. Contain both and the job was done. The reward was everything Ilvara had accumulated, which, given that she was a high priestess who had been running an operation in this cavern, was probably worth the attempt.


Grim arithmetic. We did the calculation and arrived at the same answer.


I spent the hour before the assault doing the thing that was useful and quiet and required no discussion. I placed a zone of Darkness behind our position — not at the entrance, not anywhere Ilvara might notice the edge of it and understand what was coming. Behind us. A fallback. A place to pull someone if the line broke. Nobody asked me about it. That was fine. Rope you've coiled is still rope.


The scene with Asha Vandry ended the way all negotiations end when neither party trusts the other but both parties need the same thing: a tentative and mutual willingness to proceed. She had not addressed me once. I had watched everything.


I knew where the shadows fell in the cavern ahead. I knew the shape of what we were walking into. That was enough.


## Grygum — Planning the Assassination of Ilvara
The plan began, as most plans do, with me laying out what I had.


"It's a… the spell is Glyph of Warding," I said, because someone had asked and I'd been waiting for the right moment to explain it. "You inscribe it in a particular place, and then when somebody triggers it by moving across it, it does its thing." Simple. Elegant. Impersonal in the way that traps have to be impersonal, because if you think too hard about who's walking into it, you hesitate on the inscription.


Then I kept going, because the moment called for it. I'd been carrying these spells like coins in a purse I hadn't spent yet, waiting for exactly the kind of situation where spending them right could change everything.


"So I also have another spell I haven't had a chance to use yet called the Dust of Suleiman," I said, and I'll admit there was something satisfying about laying it on the table. "Which I can cast at a 120-foot distance. It creates a 20-foot sphere, and anything inside this sphere takes 20 points of damage per turn."


Daz was paying attention. I could tell.


So I kept going.


"Finally, I now have Phantasmal Killer."


"What's that?" Daz said. "Sounds fantastic."


It does sound fantastic. That's part of why I like it. "120-foot range," I said. "It says you tap into the nightmares of a creature you can see and create an illusory manifestation of its deepest fears. They become frightened if they fail a wisdom saving throw, and if they fail the saving throw in each turn — and it can last up to a minute — then they take 4 die 10 psychic damage."


A spell that reaches into something's mind and pulls out what it's afraid of. Bahamut didn't teach me that one, exactly, but I don't think he'd object. Some fears are just.


Then came the part I'd been dreading saying out loud.


"So these powerful spells I've been talking about… they're all 4th level, and I only have one fourth level slot. I can only cast one."


Three keys. One lock. The planning was, in a sense, just a very elaborate way of deciding which door we most needed to open.


I took notes in my head, the way I always do. Three options. One shot. Everything downstream of that decision.


I'm not too proud to ask for help when the terrain is unfamiliar. The politics of this cavern — the power structures, the hierarchy of what needed to die in what order to make the rest of it possible — that was Asha Vandry's territory, and I knew it.


"Well, that would be good," I said. "Does Asha have any ideas?"


Asking a contemptuous drow priestess for tactical input is not the most comfortable thing I've done in the Underdark. But it's what the situation required.


Asha looked at me the way you look at someone who's just suggested something obvious. "Well," she said, and I could hear the slight effort it cost her to engage with us at all, "that's an obvious one — use it on the big heart fungus."


"Oh," I said. "I was thinking about using it on Ilvara."


She stared at me for a moment. Then, with the practiced precision of someone who has spent years cultivating disdain: "I thought you had strong allies, who had a tactical awareness and understanding how to use their magic for destructive purposes."


There it is. The sting of being corrected by someone who hates you — made worse by the possibility that she's right. I took the note. I filed it. I did not say anything I would regret.


The Heart Fungus was the key. That was her point, and she wasn't wrong. The fungus was how Ilvara kept control over the whole area. Kill the fungus and you don't just hurt Ilvara — you dismantle the architecture of her power. The Dust of Suleiman, which I had been imagining pouring down on Ilvara directly, was actually more valuable as infrastructure demolition.


She was right. I hate when that happens.


Then came the question of who could safely touch what.


"Basically pointing out," Asha said, her voice shifting to the register of someone delivering information they find tedious, "that if Thorin and Grygum grab it — I think they were the two that went to visit, what's her name, right? — yeah, you two probably don't want to be near it, but everybody else is fine."


Whatever we'd picked up in Gracklstugh in the Whorestone Caverns when we visited the pocket dimension, had left a marker on us. The mushroom artifact could exploit it. Good to know before I walked up and tried to destroy it with my hands.


Zalthir, bless him, raised the broader concern about spores. He'd been thinking about it — we all had.


Asha's response was theatrical. "Oh, no, no, oh, oh, oh — you think that you'll get infected by some spores and turned into Zuggtmoy demon spores and become mindless creatures that are under the whim of Ilvara?"


"That was on the list, sure," Zalthir said.


"You worry about these things a lot."


"Yes," Zalthir said.


"We do, yeah," I agreed.


Because we had seen what that looked like. Zalthir gestured toward Daz. "I mean, — look at him! He's already infected. I don't want to be infected."


There's nothing more clarifying than having a visual aid. The spores weren't theoretical. We'd seen what happened when someone stopped worrying about them.


Daz smoothed it over, as Daz does — explaining that our caution came from unfamiliarity with mushroom-related side effects, and that asking for expert guidance was tactically sound rather than ignorant. Asha was, apparently, briefly impressed.


"Well put, well put," she said. "This was just a test. Don't touch me."


The relief on Daz's face was worth something. The absurdity of Asha trying to pretend she'd been testing us rather than ranting was worth more.


Then she delivered the actual intelligence — through Daz, not through me, because I had apparently lost her respect at some point and she was going to make sure I knew it.


"My dear boy," she said, and then looked at me with contempt, and then turned to Daz. "Tell the Dragonborn that the big heart fungus will not actually cause any fungal disease, other than the usual poisoning and pain and suffering and breathing difficulties. The particular spore illness only originates from Ilvara's spores and from her mushroom."


She paused.


"I believe."


I believe. Two words that undermined everything she'd just said with such authority. I stored them carefully. *She believes.* Not *she knows.* Two very different categories of information, and I've been in enough bad situations to know the difference matters.


While we were deep in the logistics, Asha let something slip that I hadn't expected: genuine exasperation.


"The only three people who are no longer captured by her spores are myself and Jorlan," she said. "Me, because I have diligently kept the faith to Lolth, and have rejected all of these claims of the bride heresy, and Jorlan, because apparently he really hates Ilvara for something or other."


And then she kept going.


"It's very awkward, really, to be stuck with these two. I mean, they used to have an affair, it used to be this whole thing, and then she broke up with him, and then he was bitter because she broke up with him, and now I've had to be in the middle of these two for the last few months. Do you have any idea how aggravating that is?"


I couldn't help it. "Oh, man," I said. "These relationships are so complicated."


"I agree!" she said, with a warmth she'd shown none of us since we'd met her. "Like, you would think that in the midst of the abyss with the demons, everyone could get over their relationship issues, but no, no, no, no, no — Jorlan still is complaining that Ilvara… Ilvara this and Ilvara that."


There's something very human about that. Very drow, I suppose. The world is ending and someone's still nursing a broken heart in the middle of it. I took notes. Not on the tactics — on Asha, who was more person than I'd given her credit for, even if she refused to speak to me directly.


Glabbagool nearly ended the meeting early.


At some point, Asha noticed the blinking eyes emerging from the ooze, and the question of cover story became suddenly urgent. Thorin solved it with characteristic practicality.


"Will Glabbagool be offended if we refer to him as a familiar? I think that's probably the easiest way to just explain it away," he muttered. Then the dwarf introduced Glabbagool as Daz's familiar.


Asha considered this. Her expression shifted from alarmed to — and I watched this happen in real time — impressed. "An ooze familiar," she said, looking at Daz now with something approaching respect. I could tel that she's now even more impressed with Daz, for securing the services of an ooze familiar."


Glabbagool blinked. Probably didn't mind. I made a mental note that if we survived this, Glabbagool deserved something for the indignity of being called a familiar, even if he didn't seem to mind.


The spore servants were the last piece I needed to understand.


"What manner of creatures be they?" I asked.


"They are the drow of our party whom have totally succumbed to Ilvara's power," Asha said. "There were those who foolishly believed Ilvara's claims about how the bride was Lolth's further manifestation, and who embraced the spores and the ecstasy that came through them."


People. Hollowed out. Not monsters dragged in from somewhere else, but people who had made a choice — a terrible choice, maybe a seduced choice — and were now walking around as the consequence of it. The glyph I was about to inscribe would not know the difference between them and anyone else who crossed it. I noted that too.


The glyph went down on the bridge.


I spent the necessary time on the inscription. Stone holds runes differently than wood — you have to press harder, commit earlier. While I was working, I heard myself say: "I don't know how to inscribe a glyph that will only kill the drow that we don't like."


Which is the honest problem with a blunt instrument. The glyph doesn't have opinions. It just detonates.


And I remember that we had picked Asha over Jorlan.


"Okay," I said. "And then, I guess the trigger is — anyone approaching from the side that Jorlan is on."


Not a perfect solution. A workable one. Jorlan was not our ally, even if he hated Ilvara. We'd made our choice between the two of them, and the glyph would reflect it. I finished the inscription and straightened up. The bridge was now a threshold. Everything on the far side of it was already committed to a detonation it didn't know about yet.


Thorin was working through the problem of his own usefulness, which I respected.


"What do you all think the best thing for me to do is? Because I'm kind of a brawler, and it sounds like we're not really going to get close."


Daz talked through the phases — the opening volley, the depletion of spell slots, the point at which the brawlers would need to go in. Thorin thought about his darts. "Yeah, my darts will only — are not great for range. This is… I gotta figure something out."


"Are you good with other ranged weapons?" I asked. "I can lend — I can lend you my light crossbow."


It's a small thing. But everyone needs a way to contribute when it starts. I handed it over and hoped he wouldn't need it.


We watched Ilvara from the corridor near the pools.


She was on an elevated platform. Corrupted. The fungal growth had changed her in ways that were specific and terrible — the scourge still in her hand, but everything else altered. She was preoccupied with the mushroom token. She didn't know we were watching. The Heart Fungus pulsed between us and her, enormous and obscene, the mechanism of her control.


I looked at her and I thought: Phantasmal Killer reaches into nightmares. What does she dream about? What would an illusory manifestation of her deepest fears look like?


Then Zalthir said: "I can try to grab Ilvara and drop her into that, such that you're hurting both of them, and then the rest of you can fireball or whatever from there."


And there it was. The final shape of it. Zalthir teleports in, grapples, drops her into the sphere of damage. The Dust of Suleiman burns the Heart Fungus. Daz fireballs the overlap. Thorin holds the line with my crossbow and whatever else he has. The glyph waits on the bridge for whoever tries to reinforce her.


One 4th-level slot. I'd made my choice. The Dust of Suleiman on the fungus, because Asha was right and the infrastructure was more important than the satisfying image of Ilvara's nightmares eating her alive.


I took notes on the whole plan, the way I always do. Not on paper. In my head. Every piece placed, every spell accounted for.


Now we just had to survive the execution.


## Thorin — The Battle of the Heart Fungus
The plan was always going to start without me.


That's not self-pity — that's just terrain. Ilvara is sixty feet up, Zalthir has wings, and I'm standing on a bridge over a pit that smells like the inside of a dying thing. The smart play is ranged. I know this. I made peace with it before the fight started. Mostly.


I watch Zalthir vanish — just gone, teleported straight to her — and then there's the crack of his fists finding purchase, once, twice, thirty-one damage in the space of a breath. That's the opening salvo. That's the plan working. I note it, feel the familiar surge of wanting to be in the middle of it, and then she melts.


She just *melts*. Into the floor. Into the fungus. The grapple breaks like it was nothing, and she reforms somewhere else in the cavern screaming, and I think: *okay. Different kind of opponent.*


The Heart Fungus has been sitting in the middle of the cavern this whole time, pulsing, and I have been treating it as terrain. That's a mistake I revise immediately when it unleashes a cloud of spores that hits Zalthir so hard he has to climb ten feet straight up just to breathe. The thing has opinions about us being here. Fine. It can get in line.


Then the spore servants come up out of the ground — four of them, former Velkynvelve guards, shambling and wrong — and now the cavern is full in a way that starts to feel personal. Ilvara is rebuilding the math of the fight in real time. She's fast. I respect it. I don't like it.


I don't have a ranged option worth mentioning. Dawnbringer is a sword, and I am a dwarf who was raised by giants, which means my relationship to range is "how far can I throw this rock." So someone lends me a crossbow, and I take it, because I'd rather be in the fight badly than out of it entirely. I hold it like a man who knows exactly how to hold a sword.


And then I see Jorlan.


He's across the cavern, bitter-eyed, nursing whatever wound his pride has taken since Ilvara discarded him. He's armed. He's furious. He's also standing exactly twenty feet from the Glyph of Warding that Grygum spent real patience setting into the stone of the bridge.


Jorlan doesn't know about the glyph. Jorlan just needs to take about four steps in the right direction.


I put the crossbow down.


I have been thinking in terrain and leverage since before I could name those words for what they were. Giants negotiate through position — you stand where you stand, and the ground under your feet says everything your mouth doesn't need to. Jorlan is a man who has been humiliated by someone he loved, left broken on the far side of that love, and he's been stewing in it so long the rot has gone all the way through. That makes him predictable. Rage that personal has a vector.


I just have to give him a target.


"Come get it, idiot!"


It's not eloquent. Giants aren't eloquent, and neither am I, and neither is this situation. What it is, is *direct*.


Jorlan charges.


He comes straight onto the bridge, straight onto the glyph, and the detonation is immediate and total — 21 damage, one spore servant gone to nothing, another barely holding itself together, and the Heart Fungus itself shuddering from the force of it. The bridge holds. The math shifts.


I didn't expect it to work that cleanly. I'll take it.


Now I have Dawnbringer in hand — the real hand, the right hand, the one that knows what it's doing — and Jorlan is on the bridge in front of me, wounded and furious and still standing, which is a problem I can work with. The blade catches some ambient light from somewhere and turns it into something brighter, something that has no business existing this deep underground, and I close the distance.


The first hit lands true. Fourteen damage, and I roll through the Sap, put him off-balance, give him disadvantage on whatever's coming back at me. Jorlan staggers. He doesn't fall.


The second swing goes wide. I know it the moment the blade leaves my hands at the wrong angle — too high, overcommitted, Jorlan's AC is 18 and I'm rolling a 2 and there's nothing in the world that fixes a 2. The Precision Attack doesn't find it. Jorlan is still there, staggered but not broken, which means he's still angry, which means we are not done here.


Then a spore walker climbs up behind me, because of course it does, and the first swing misses clean — the thing reaches up and just swings into nothing, which is the best that can be said about it — but the second one connects. Five damage. A glancing thing. Thorin, son of the Ember Vanguard, flanked by a dead man's fungal corpse while the fight rages in all directions around him.


I adjust my footing.


Behind me, deeper in the cavern, Grygum is grinding the Heart Fungus down with something that sounds like the word *Suleiman* and delivers twenty force damage per turn like a promise. The thing shudders. Good. Let it shudder. And then Daz opens up with a Fireball shaped so precisely around his allies that it catches Ilvara and her precious mushroom icon in the center of it, 24 damage worth of fire finding her even through her save, and the icon smolders.


The mushroom is burning. Ilvara is bleeding. The math has changed.


Jorlan is still in front of me, and Dawnbringer is still in my hand.


# Chapter 47 A Spore-Filled Finale


## Zalthir — The Confrontation with Ilvara
He announced it like it was barely worth saying.
*I'm just gonna try annoying her then.*
That was the plan. That was the whole plan. Ilvara Mizzrym, high priestess of House Mizzrym, Zuggtmoy's beloved instrument, corrupted vessel of a demon lord of fungal nightmare — and I was going to *annoy* her. The eldritch tendrils of the tattoo were already unreeling from my arm before the words finished leaving my mouth.
The cavernous space above the heart fungus was dark and vast and exactly the kind of silence I prefer to operate in. I crossed it fast, using the reach of the tentacles to close the distance before she had a chance to process what I was doing. The first strikes landed. Sixteen damage, the grapple secured.
*She's 16 damage and grappled, right?*
She was. I was holding onto her. That was the important part.
I do not, as a rule, enjoy monologue. I leave that to Daz, who has the cheekbones for it. What I enjoy is the moment when a thing is in your hands and you have not yet decided to let go of it. That moment. That is the thing I came to the monastery for, even if I did not know it when I arrived, even if I spent the first three years thinking I was there to learn to fight.
I dragged her up. Forty feet. The stone floor dropped away beneath us, and the enormous obscene mass of the heart fungus shrank below, and Ilvara was — for a moment — entirely subject to gravity and to me.
Then the dice agreed with me. Twice.
*Crit.*
I do not celebrate at the table. It is not my custom. But the second one surprised even me, and I recall something warm and undignified moving through my chest that I chose not to name.
*Oh, wow. Another crit.*
Forty feet up, gripping a corrupted high priestess, delivering consecutive critical strikes — and then I let her go. That was the point. The drop was always the point. The grapple was just delivery.
She hit the ground.
And then she looked up at me.
I have trained for years to read danger in the environment before I read it in the faces of creatures. The shape of the tunnel, the angle of a weapon, the particular quality of silence before something moves. I am rarely caught by what happens *after* the decisive action.
But Ilvara Mizzrym, bleeding, furious, struck from forty feet of free fall, still had something left. She reached into whatever dark place Zuggtmoy had hollowed out inside her and she *pushed* — not against my body, not against my grip, but against the part of me that the training does not fully reach.
*Roll a DC-14 Wisdom saving throw.*
Well. That didn't work.
The fear was immediate and specific and entirely unwanted. Not a general dread — not the crawling ambient horror of the Underdark that you learn to fold into background noise. This was targeted. This said: *she is down there, and you will not approach her, and you will not approach her, and you will not approach her.* Repeated. Insistent.
*You are frightened. Which is very convenient, because you cannot now approach her.*
*Indeed.*
I am a monk. My entire tactical vocabulary is built around being adjacent to the problem. Range is something other people deal with. Range is Daz's whole personality. I hit things. I grapple things. I drag things into the air and then drop them from inconvenient heights. All of that requires *proximity.*
And Ilvara's legendary reaction had, in one economical move, made proximity impossible.
I stayed where I was. Forty feet up. The frightened condition is not subtle — it is not a suggestion. I turned it over, the way you turn over a rule that has been applied against you, looking for the seam where the letter and the spirit diverge.
The spirit seems to be that I should do nothing to become closer to her.
Right.
*Alright. Fine.*
I am supposed to apply the rule honestly, even against my own interest. That is the monastic training operating at the level of self-adjudication, or so I was told during the years I spent thinking I was there to learn to fight. I spent a lot of time dismissing lessons that turned out to be useful. I am still working through the implications of that.
So I stayed up. I did not approach.
And the heart fungus, which had apparently been paying attention, chose this moment to remind me that fifteen feet of reach is a very generous number when the thing reaching lives on the floor and I have been hovering at the ceiling being useless.
It found me. The blow was enormous — the kind of damage that lands differently when you have no immediate recourse, when you cannot immediately close back in and return the favor. I deflected twelve of the bludgeoning. The rest I accepted. The poison damage I noted and filed away beside the indignity.
*Thanks, buddy.*
I said it quietly. I doubt anyone heard. It felt like the appropriate response — dry, clipped, and entirely sincere in its lack of sentiment. The heart fungus had earned its acknowledgment. It had done exactly what the situation called for, which is the most I ask of anything.
I remained where I was. Frightened. Grounded by something I could not approach. Watching from the ceiling while the rest of them worked.
It was not where I intended to be.


## Thorin — The Heart Fungus and the Drow Betrayal
The Heart Fungus stood at the center of the cavern like something that thought it had time. Massive, pulsing, ringed with spore-choked air and difficult terrain between me and it — a whole careful geography of annoyance. Ilvara had retreated into its shadow, and everything that mattered in this fight was on the wrong side of a cave full of fungal servants and an insect swarm that hadn't arrived yet but was coming, the way bad things always are in the Underdark. Coming, and then suddenly here.
I've been in enough fights now to know the shape of a bottleneck. The cavern was doing most of Ilvara's work for her — keeping us spread out, keeping me in particular at range from the thing I wanted to hit. I am, in my heart, a dwarf who wants to walk up to the problem and apply Dawnbringer to it until the problem stops. The Underdark keeps presenting me with long rooms.
So I descended from the elevated walkway and pushed through the terrain toward the heart fungus, and I did the sensible thing first: I asked if there was anything I needed to know about it.
"Is there anything I need to... anything I know about the fungus in terms of, like, is it, like, resistant to stuff or whatever?"
"It's resistant to the usual stuff that funguses are resistant to, but if you hit it hard enough, it should die."
Grygum, from somewhere behind me, helpful as always: "It's not resistant to swords."
And then the DM, with the particular cadence of a man setting up something he was proud of: "It actually is doubly not resistant to radiant damage."
Dawnbringer was already in my hand. She'd been in my hand since we walked in. She's not the sort of sword that waits politely in a scabbard when there's something this obviously in need of being hit with radiant light.
The answer I was looking for had been at the end of my arm the entire time.
I struck. And then struck again. The heart fungus wilted.
One round. That was all it got.
"And so you kill it. So it wilts."
"I do what I can."
There was a pause — the particular pause of a dungeon master looking at a carefully prepared battle map and making peace with what just happened.
"I spent so much time creating this icon."
"I know, it was beautiful," Grygum said.
"It was pretty," Zalthir agreed.
"I liked it very much, Gustatus."
The party offered their condolences, and I accepted them on the fungus's behalf. It had been a fine-looking icon. Big. Pulsing. Lovingly rendered. It deserved a longer fight than it got. These things happen. Dawnbringer doesn't negotiate, and she doesn't do gradual.
Then Ilvara cast Insect Plague.
The swarm hit the cavern entrance like a wall of sound and motion — a biblical chittering that shredded the fungal minions instantly, all thirteen hit points of them, gone before they could do anything useful. And then Grygum said what we were all thinking:
"Oh, how about Glabbagool?"
Twenty-six points of damage. The grey ooze took twenty-six points of damage from a spell he had nothing to do with, while he was hovering at the edge of the chaos doing his best to look like he was following the conversation. He'd been trying to participate. He had eyes extended toward us and everything. He survived, which is the important thing, but still — twenty-six points for a sentient ooze who just wanted to belong. The Underdark is not kind to bystanders.
I filed that away. I would be checking on Glabbagool when this was over.
Then Jorlan moved.
The scarred drow warrior had been fighting with us — or near us, which in a battle this chaotic amounted to roughly the same thing — and then Ilvara appeared wounded, and something older than tactics took over. He broke from position and charged toward her across the swarm-infested terrain, and the name that came out of him was not a battle cry.
"ILVARA"
It came out like a wound reopening. Not rage exactly — rage is clean, rage has direction. This was something uglier and more honest than rage.
I had an opportunity attack. I stood there with Dawnbringer in hand and thought about it, which is not usually how I operate, but this particular situation had enough moving parts to give even me pause. He'd attacked us. That was true. He'd attacked us first, back at the beginning of this whole catastrophe, and you don't just set that aside because the political geometry shifted. On the other hand, he was now charging Ilvara, who we needed dead, which made him functionally useful at exactly the moment when we needed useful things.
"So, what happens if he kills her and we don't? Does that change anything? Does he get, like, first dibs on booty or something? And by booty, I mean, like... they used to be Lover's Booty, I mean, like... the goods."
The table responded to this with exactly the energy it deserved.
The DM explained the situation — Ilvara had dumped Jorlan when he became Scarface, because Drow society rewards beauty and punishes everything else, because Drow society is a whole list of things I find exhausting — and Zalthir, deadpan as ever, offered the only appropriate summary:
"The tides have turned."
Eight on the die. Eight says I do the sensible thing.
"Alright, so 8 says I do the sensible thing. Which I guess is let them go and let the enemies... let the girls fight."
I stepped aside and let Jorlan pass.
It's a strange thing, choosing not to act. Thorin Giantfriend, raised by creatures who settle disagreements by picking up boulders, standing in a fungal cave in the Underdark while a scarred drow with a grudge and an old love charges through an insect swarm toward his former partner. There's something that feels almost like mercy in it. There's something that feels almost like tactics. I'm not sure they're different.
The truth is I was mostly just relieved someone else was handling that particular problem. There is a specific kind of Underdark drama — the faction grudge, the Drow lover's quarrel, the old wound dressed up as ideology — that I have no useful purchase on. I understand hills. I understand stone. I understand the weight of a sword and the arc it needs to travel to hit something. I do not understand six layers of drow politics wrapped around a heartbreak, and I am not ashamed to say so.
I'm just sitting here watching them trade blows like a tennis match.
Sometimes the most useful thing a fighter can do is stay out of someone else's reckoning.


## Grygum — Chaos in the Fungal Cavern
The insect plague hit like a wave breaking through a cracked hull — one moment the cavern was loud with shouting and the clatter of steel, and the next it was *screaming*. Not from us. From everything between us and Ilvara.
I felt the swarm before I saw it. A wall of noise, of biting, chittering fury, the kind of sound that bypasses the thinking part of the brain and goes straight to the older, smaller part that just says *run*. I did not run. I want to note that. But I was also very, very aware of exactly how many insects were currently conducting a theological inquiry into the structural integrity of my skin.
Ilvara had conjured it at the cave entrance, a sphere of plague large enough to catch the whole middle ground between us — me, Daz, and, as I registered with a cold sinking feeling, Glabbagool. Our faithful, wobbling, occasionally conversational gelatinous cube companion, who had been attempting to participate in the fight with the earnest commitment of someone who was very new to fighting and very sincere about it.
"Oh, how about Glabbagool?"
That was me. That was the question I asked first, before I assessed my own position, before I checked the tactical map for who was flanking whom. Glabbagool. Twenty-six points of damage, I was told. The cube had survived, churning and trembling in the swarm, but the number landed in my chest like a stone. Twenty-six points. We had not brought a gelatinous cube this far through the Underdark to lose him to biting insects conjured by a woman who had traded her dignity to a fungal demon lord.
Bahamut rewards the protection of the vulnerable. I'm not certain gelatinous cubes were what the Platinum Dragon had in mind. But I took notes on the principle, and the principle applies.


It was around this time that Thorin descended from the elevated walkways.
Thorin drove Dawnbringer into the Heart Fungus. One round. *Boy, I had so much prepared for the fungus.* I continued.


There was a moment — a genuine moment of sheepishness — when the fight churned on and bodies began dropping around us and I did an honest accounting of who had fallen and what we owed them, and I said:
"If anybody is too saddened by the loss of any of our minions, I do have a Revivify spell that I could use to bring one of them back."
Someone shouted immediately. Not our minions. *Her* minions. The fallen fungal servants belonged to Ilvara, not to us. Of course they did.
"We used to have so many people traveling with us," I said, "I forgot."
This is, I think, a reasonable accounting of what happens when you spend extended time in the Underdark with an expanding roster of companions, allies, prisoners, and one sentient gelatinous cube. The bookkeeping gets away from you. At some point the question of *whose* minion has collapsed becomes genuinely difficult to answer on the fly. I'm not embarrassed by this. I take notes. The notes had just not caught up with the situation.


I tried a Guiding Bolt at Ilvara. The bolt missed. I note this without rancor. Ilvara, battered from her forty-foot drop courtesy of Zalthir's extraordinary grip-and-release approach to aerial combat, was still moving, still conjuring, still calling down divine fire on Daz and Nym Duskryn with the focused fury of someone who had not yet accepted that this was over. The bridge cracked under her. Thorin's work. She leapt clear anyway.
A miss that bought time for the battle to grow worse before it got better. These are the outcomes you accept and continue from.


"Just when you thought everything was under control..."
The GM said this with a tone I recognized immediately. The tone of a person who has not, in fact, run out of material.
"Are there 3 more unnamed creatures?"
"5 Dark Elves, it looks like? 5 Drow?" Zalthir processed this with his characteristic meditative calm. "That's awesome."
"This is turning into the 12 days of Christmas," I said.
Five drow warriors emerging from the cavern shadows. The insect bloom still hanging in the air. The new arrivals were already inside the poison cloud's range. I could see it in their posture — the particular quality of someone who has committed to a dramatic entrance and is now realizing they have stepped into a catastrophe they had no part in making.
"Dark Elves!" I added, in a singing tone. The twelve days of Christmas. Five of them. Very irritated about the poison. Really had hoped to avoid the poison.
It's tough being a minion. This is a truth that applies universally and transcends factional allegiance. You show up to someone else's fight, you discover someone else's insect plague, and you spend the rest of the encounter just trying to manage consequences you didn't generate.


And then Ilvara was still standing. Still radiating darkness and spite and the unmistakable spiritual signature of someone who has been promised the Underdark by a demon lord and is not prepared to surrender that claim without fully expressing her grievances.
"Ilvara seems like she's still trouble," I said, "so I'm going to cast a guiding bolt in that direction."
I was amused by the Jorlan-Ilvara drama. I want to be clear about that. The romantic betrayal. The scarring. The fury. The way he had broken from us to pursue her through an active insect plague. It was genuinely interesting. I filed it, appreciated it, and continued.
I am still going to go ahead and attack her.
The bolt found her this time. Radiant light. Clean and bright and final. Ilvara — Zuggtmoy's instrument, the corrupted high priestess of House Mizzrym, the reason we had been prisoners in the first place — folded and went still, and then, because the Underdark does not allow even death to be dignified, she did not simply fall.
She *erupted*. A cloud of poisonous spores, billowing outward from what had been a person. A woman who had traded herself to a fungal demon lord, and what was left was biology, not identity. Not even a body. Just spores.
"Not ideal," I said, immediately. "How big is the cloud?"
I was glad I was standing back. This is the whole of my commentary on the death of Ilvara Mizzrym. Pragmatic wisdom. I was standing back. The cloud was noted. I took notes.


## Daz — The Drow Reinforcements
The first bolt hit my shield ward before I'd consciously decided to cast it.
That's the thing about Shield — it's a reaction, which means the body decides before the mind does. One moment I was watching three Drow warriors step out of concealment with the particular ease of people who think they've caught something interesting in their sights. The next moment arcane force was already flaring around me, and the bolt that would have found my ribs clattered away into the dark instead. My hand was still raised. My breathing had not changed.
I spent a moment noting the look on their faces. That specific expression — the slight recalibration when the target doesn't go down. I find it useful to catalogue.
*Three Drow warriors. Crossbow formation. Tactical positions chosen in advance. Someone prepared for this.*
The cost was one spell slot. The benefit was that I was still standing. These are acceptable terms.
Then Kaelira's hand closed around my arm like a vice.
"Come with me if you want to live, you idiot."
I went. Not gracefully — she was hauling me northeast through the chittering wall of it while the poisonous gas did its work on both of us. She had my arm, she had the direction, and she had strong opinions about my continued survival that she was enforcing without my consent. Nym was already there, waiting.
Asha saw them at almost the same moment I registered where we'd ended up. Her hand moved toward her weapon. The energy of a zealot who has decided she is being betrayed — that tight, righteous recognition — came off her like heat.
"Who are you? You must be traitors! Traitors to Lolth!"
There was a window of perhaps two seconds before this became catastrophic.
"No, these are friends of Lolth, and they're here to help."
The words came out smooth. No hesitation, no announcement, no explanation. I have been maintaining a Lolth-follower identity with Asha Vandree for long enough that the fiction has its own momentum — you learn to stop thinking of it as lying and start thinking of it as consistent characterization. Asha is a Lolth-devotee; she has a ready-made slot in her worldview for "Lolth-aligned drow who are here to help." I put the Duskryn sisters in that slot and moved on. The lie barely costs anything when you're already committed to the architecture.
Grygum, at some point in this sequence, observed that the situation was "accurately described" by Asha's characterization of me as an insane mage who had converted to Lolthism. He meant it kindly. Or as kindly as Grygum observes anything, which is with the steady, clear-eyed precision of a man keeping accurate records.
*What's important? That she thinks it, or that it's real?*
I know what's real. I know what I've left behind and what I haven't and what Menzoberranzan made me and what the Underdark has cost me and what I believe now, privately, about slavery and free people and the basis of any society. I know all of that. But right now, in this cavern, Asha thinks I serve Lolth, and that belief is load-bearing.
So we let it stand.
Nym Duskryn had been watching all of this with the particular expression of someone who is calculating whether their contract is still viable. She had been sent — she and Kaelira both — to extract me. Deliver the package intact. Get paid. It is a simple arrangement and I had been making it complicated since the moment she arrived.
"Can you please keep your head down and out of combat? We only get paid if you live."
I find transactional arguments interesting when they're offered as though they should be persuasive to the person whose life is being treated as currency. The logic runs: *Daz, you should hide, because we get paid when you live.* The invisible premise is that I should care about whether Nym gets paid. I don't, particularly. What I care about is that Thorin and Grygum and Zalthir are in active combat with a corrupted high priestess and a field of biting insects and what appears to be an inexhaustible supply of Drow reinforcements, and the idea of standing in a shadow while that happens is — I want to find a precise word — *unacceptable.*
"I am not gonna leave. Everyone here... to fight for me without me also participating. I don't give a damn if you get paid. The best way for you to get paid, then, is to continue the fight and prevent all of us from dying."
There it was. Said. Not eloquent — I am capable of elegance, and that was not it. But it was accurate, and it was what I meant, and Nym heard it correctly because her expression shifted from exasperated professionalism to something more pointed.
She turned away from me.
"Can I kill your brother?"
Delivered flat. A genuine inquiry, redirected frustration finding the nearest available target with a professional's efficiency.
"He was always the dumber of the ones, and if I understood correctly, he's covered in fungus?"
What I had not fully accounted for was the T'sarran soldiers themselves, who were busy at the tunnel mouth having an argument that I can only describe as profoundly unhelpful given the circumstances.
"I thought you said this was going to be an easy kill."
"And you don't have a Dispel Magic."
"No, I don't have a Dispel—"
"So I have to go through this. I don't know what else to tell you."
Elite drow soldiers. Trained. Armored. Standing at the threshold of a crisis they had presumably accepted a contract to resolve, now stymied by a wall of insects and a mage with insufficient spell preparation. There's a category of failure I find almost aesthetically interesting — not the grand collapse, but the petty logistical one. They had the firepower. They had the numbers. What they did not have was Dispel Magic, and so they stood in the tunnel debating whether to charge while a 4th-level fireball was introduced into the conversation.
The mage launched a lightning bolt into the cavern with real commitment. It hit nothing. Just open air and ambition.
The question of what spell to use is, in the end, always a question of geometry. You are managing a volume. You are thinking about who is inside it, who is outside it, and how much of what you need is inside relative to the damage you're prepared to accept on the edges. An Evoker — and I am an Evoker, trained in Menzoberranzan where the destructive arts were considered the only magic worth taking seriously — learns to carve exceptions into area spells. The ability is called Sculpt Spells, and it means I can reach into a Fireball and pull specific individuals out of harm's way, leaving the fire to do its work on everything else in the radius.
Glabbagool — our sentient, gentle, luminously good-natured ooze companion who had been, from what I could tell, simply trying to participate in the ambient conversation of the battle — had already taken twenty-six points of damage from Ilvara's plague and survived. He doesn't deserve what this cavern keeps throwing at him. I identified the exclusion carefully.
I cast Fireball in a shaped arc around everything I wanted to keep.
The cavern went very bright for a moment.
The Drow warriors who had been irritated about the Insect Plague were no longer irritated about the Insect Plague. The mage was still standing when the smoke cleared, barely, and she had the presence of mind to deliver her parting observation:
"The matron mother of House T'sarran will hear about this."
She was not wrong. That is probably going to matter later. I filed it accordingly.
The lightning bolt that followed was not, technically, my problem. Jorlan Duskryn went staggering across the cavern floor, and Nym's professional frustrations had found their outlet, and I remained exactly where I had said I would remain.
Not hiding. Not extracted. Not managed.
The feared mage of the Ember Vanguard, standing in the smoke of his own fireball, surrounded by people who were fighting because he refused to leave.
I find, somewhat to my own surprise, that this is what purpose feels like.


## Zalthir — Ilvara's Desperate Stand
The frightened condition is, in theory, simple. You avoid the source of your fear and you wait for it to pass. Clean. Mechanical. Zalthir understood it immediately — and hated it with the particular hatred reserved for things that cannot be argued with.
He had opened this fight by *picking Ilvara up*. Used the Eldritch Claw Tattoo to wrap tentacles around her, hauled her forty feet into the air, hit her twice in the ribs during the ascent, and dropped her. She'd hit stone like something that had stopped mattering. The sequence had lasted perhaps four seconds. He had delivered it with the emotional register of someone pushing a chair under a table.
And then she looked at him. And something in that look — something Zuggtmoy-deep, something that had nothing to do with Ilvara Mizzrym's personal opinion of Zalthir and everything to do with the demon lord wearing her face — *landed*. The fear dropped into him like a stone into still water. No noise, just depth. And suddenly he could not go forward. Could not cross the distance. Every trained instinct read the direction toward Ilvara as simply, absolutely wrong.
He stood there and accepted it.
*I am supposed to be trying to not be near her. Right.*
Not a question. Just the rule, stated plainly, applied against his own interest the way you check a knot even when you know you tied it correctly. He didn't fight the condition. He didn't look for angles. The spirit of it was that he shouldn't close on her, and he knew the spirit of it, and so he didn't. The discipline is self-administered. Always has been.
It was still humiliating.
"How do I not be frightened?" He had asked, because he wanted to know the timeline. One turn. He just needed to wait one turn.
"I'm a monk."
This came out in response to someone noting — correctly, irritatingly — that his entire contribution to a fight was predicated on being in contact with the thing he was supposed to be destroying. The observation landed without malice. It was just the fact on the table. *Yeah. I mean, how do you slap?* No answer to that. A monk frozen by fear is a monk who has no tools. He had the Eldritch Claw Tattoo — fifteen feet of reach — but the distance between him and Ilvara was not fifteen feet. She was busy reshaping the battlefield around herself, throwing up fungal growth, buying room she knew she needed. Even injured. Even howling.
She was still muttering. Something about Zuggtmoy. Something about the bride, and chaos, and the mayhem to come. She said it in the tone of someone who genuinely believed it, which was the worst kind. Not a threat. A prophecy she'd already made her peace with.
He watched from across the cavern and could not move toward her and filed it away and waited.
One turn is a long time when you have nothing to do in it.
The T'sarran mage was still standing. Barely. Daz had already run an empowered fireball through him; the man was singed at the edges and blinking with the stunned expression of someone who had entered this fight expecting an easy kill and received instead a detailed education in why that assumption was wrong. He had fired a lightning bolt into the cavern when he arrived — a dramatic entrance, the kind with real commitment — and hit nothing. Just open air and ambition.
*I shall punch the mage.*
Zalthir said this without particular enthusiasm. He teleported across the battlefield — shadow-step, the distance closing in the space between one breath and the next — and hit the mage once, and that was enough. The man went down. Zalthir straightened.
The fear cleared at the end of his turn, the way it had been promised to. He noted this without celebration.
Ilvara was still on the far side of the cavern, still howling, her voice ragged with the particular desperation of someone who has run out of futures but not yet run out of throat. *Vengeance. Zuggtmoy. The bride.* All of it tumbling out between strikes and retreats. Even cornered, she filled the space with prophecy rather than offers. He could respect the discipline of that, abstractly. She wasn't going to beg.
The mage's body settled on the cavern floor. Behind Zalthir, someone — Grygum, probably — was already moving forward. Someone else was dealing with the insect swarm at the entrance. The fight was contracting toward its end the way fights do, the radius shrinking as each threat resolved.
*We barely knew you, man.* He didn't say this aloud. Just noted it, and turned toward where the remaining work was being done.


## Grygum — The Fall of Ilvara
The bolt was already building in my hand when I made the decision.
Let me back up. For most of this fight I had been in observation mode — which is a polite way of saying I was watching Jorlan Duskryn and Ilvara Mizzrym tear at each other like competing bad ideas, and finding it genuinely entertaining. You don't often get to watch your primary antagonist and a lover's grudge collide in real time. There's a whole drama there — she discarded him when he got the scar, he's been carrying that wound ever since, and now here they both are at the bottom of a ruined bridge with Zuggtmoy's rot flowering through everything she touches. I took mental notes. It's what I do.
I'm amused by the Jorlan-Ilvara drama, I thought. But I think I'm still going to go ahead and attack them.
The entertainment of watching enemies tear at each other is free. But Bahamut does not, in my experience, favor spectatorship. I have never once received a divine message that said *wait this one out, it's interesting.* He is more of a *you see the weakness, now act* kind of god. And Ilvara was still standing, still murmuring pieces of prophecy about Zuggtmoy and the bride and all the chaos to come — the kind of muttering that sounds unhinged until it turns out to be accurate, and I have seen enough of this Underdark to know better than to dismiss it. She was already something other than the woman she had been. The corruption had been working on her for a long time.
The bolt was already building.
Thorin had already toppled the bridge, sent both of them crashing to the cavern floor — classic Thorin, total commitment, Dawnbringer still humming with residual radiance the way a tuning fork hums after you strike it. Zalthir had been up and down and around, doing what Zalthir does, the entire fight a series of surgical moves delivered with the emotional register of someone sorting correspondence. But Ilvara was still here. Still muttering. Still Zuggtmoy's.
There is a version of Guiding Bolt that I think of as administrative — you point it, it works, enemy takes radiant damage, case closed. And then there is the other version, the one that happens when everything has led to this particular moment and the light in your hand feels like it has been waiting. I have seen enough corrupted things in this Underdark — the egg, the grove, the Pudding King's kingdom of rot — to know what it looks like when something has been claimed entirely by a power it should never have touched. Ilvara was not being aided by Zuggtmoy anymore. She *was* Zuggtmoy's work, walking around in a drow priestess's bones.
The bolt left my hand.
It lanced across the cavern floor in a concentrated column of radiant light and found her where she stood.
She did not die quietly. She detonated. Her body ruptured outward in a massive toxic bloom — a cloud of poisonous spores billowing through the cavern in every direction, the demonic corruption venting itself all at once in the only form it had left. Zalthir and Thorin staggered in it. I was already standing back.
Not ideal, I thought, immediately practical again. How big is the cloud?
I was glad I was standing back.
That is the whole thought. That is the entire reflection on delivering the killing blow to a demonically corrupted high priestess of Lolth in the middle of the Underdark: *not ideal, how big is the cloud, glad I was standing back.* Bahamut gets the credit for the radiance. I get credit for the positioning. We understand each other, Bahamut and I.
The cavern settled into a thick, spore-choked silence. The heart fungus was already a collapsed ruin — Thorin had seen to that in one devastating, almost insulting round, while I stood there with everything I'd prepared for it and nowhere to deploy it. *Boy, I had so much planned for the fungus.* The bridge was rubble. The poisonous bloom was already beginning to thin and drift. And somewhere in the settling quiet, Asha Vandree dropped to her knees and began murmuring prayers, her voice the only sound left — words to Lolth, rising from the wreckage, already reshaping what she'd witnessed into something her goddess could use.
Whatever theology she was building out of this moment, I filed it under *watch this space.*
The last House T'sarran warrior looked at all of it — his mage dead, his guards incinerated, Ilvara nothing but settling spores and a fading prophecy about a bride — and stepped back toward the tunnels without urgency. "We will meet again," he said. "Enjoy your victory for the moment."
I took note of that too. A threat delivered without heat is always more credible than one made in anger. I'll want to remember that name. The matron mother of House T'sarran would be hearing about this. I believe I should find out who that is before she finds out who we are.
My first real question, once the cloud was thin enough to breathe through, was about Glabbagool.


## Thorin — Aftermath at Velkynvelve
The bridge going down was my idea, technically, but I can't take full credit for it feeling satisfying. You swing at a bridge long enough, the bridge falls. That's not strategy. That's physics.
Ilvara and Jorlan both cleared the drop. I watched them hit the cavern floor and thought: *still breathing*. Then I thought about how much tunnel I'd crossed to reach that bridge, how many times the fight had relocated while I was still arriving at wherever it used to be, and I stopped tallying. Some battles you just chase until they end without you.
The fungus, at least, was mine. Clean and total. Dawnbringer lit the thing up from the first swing and it never got a second chance — big pulsating horror that must have taken the DM weeks to stat out, and it folded before anyone else could touch it. I said *I do what I can* because what else do you say when you've just ruined someone's evening in two rounds of radiant damage. Grygum said it was beautiful. Zalthir said it was pretty. I felt a little bad, in the way you feel bad about something you'd do exactly the same way again.
After that I was mostly running.
That's the honest accounting of the rest of the battle: I was running. The map spread out across half the cavern and the fight kept happening somewhere I wasn't. Drow fighting drow. Ilvara dropping, recovering, dropping again. Lightning hitting things I couldn't see from wherever I was standing. There's a version of this where I say I was *holding the center* or *covering the approach*, but the real version is that I'm a dwarf with a glowing sword in a sprawling cavern fight and at some point you stop pretending you've got angles on all of it and just pick a direction and go.
Grygum's bolt found her. Spores everywhere — the kind of death that punishes you for being nearby. I caught some of it. Zalthir caught some of it.
Dawnbringer pulsed once, steady warmth against my palm.
The spores drifted. The T'sarrans were already gone. Somewhere behind me, Grygum was probably checking on Glabbagool. Daz was probably explaining to someone why exactly this was all fine.
I stood there and let the quiet be quiet.
We'd won. I couldn't tell you yet what winning this one cost, or what it opened up ahead of us — that kind of math takes time, and the Underdark has a way of correcting your optimism before you even finish the equation. But right now the fungus was dead and the bridge was down and Ilvara Mizzrym was a cloud of dissipating spores, and Asha was still praying in the dark like she expected to be answered.
Maybe she would be. I've seen stranger things answered.
# Chapter 48 The Ember Grapple


## Thorin — The Level Eight Milestone
Something hit me, as I surveyed the battlefield. 


Not literally. Though, given how the last several months have gone, that's worth specifying. No, this was the other kind of thing — the quiet knock inside the chest that means you've crossed some threshold you didn't notice crossing. I've felt it before, in the dark, after bad fights. The body takes stock before the mind does. I was bigger somehow. More there.


"Alright, so the first thing is that we're all leveled up."


I heard myself think it before I quite understood it. Leveled up. Which meant — I ran the count back. Sloobludop. Gracklstugh. Neverlight Grove. Blingdenstone. The Pudding King. Ilvara. Everything we'd crawled through, fought through, negotiated and lied and burned our way through.


"Whoa, whoa, what?"


Daz made a noise of genuine surprise — the kind he usually reserves for things like demon princes and sentient oozes. "Oh!" And then, with that particular tone he uses when he's already processed something and moved to judgment: "We're gonna level up." He arrived at the same conclusion.


I thought about all the things I'd need to reconsider now — the new techniques settling into muscle and bone, decisions about what kind of fighter I was going to be going forward. I knew immediately how long that was going to take. Not because I couldn't decide, but because I take it seriously, and taking things seriously takes time.


"Alright, well, that's gonna take the rest of the day."


Flat statement. True statement.


I looked over at Zalthir — who was, as usual, already thinking several steps ahead of everyone else, in some private tactical space behind his eyes. Something about the way the Dragonborn operated had always impressed me, even when I didn't understand it. He builds narrow and builds sharp. Everything pointing in one direction, so that in exactly the right situation, he becomes something close to impossible.


"Is your strategy that you just build a super lopsided powers and just go with that? Actually, do you try and create some… because, like, right now, I have like, zero range ability, and I'm wondering, do I try and fix that, or do I just double down with the shit that I can't, you know?"


Zalthir considered this for a moment in the way he considers most things — like he's checking the weight of the stone before deciding where to put it.


"I find it more fun to double down with some sort of obscure specialization and then try to break the universe with it."


"I mean, you're really good at it."


Not flattery. Just accurate. I'd watched him pull the Pudding King out of its lair by sheer force of tactical geometry. That was a man who had committed to a direction and refused to apologize for it.


The honest truth is that the game punishes you for being cautious about this. Spreading your strengths thin feels responsible. Feels dwarvish, maybe — the sensible hedge, the covered flank. But enemies get bigger, and if your damage doesn't grow with them, you spend your time bouncing off. At low levels, a solid strike matters. At our level, you need to mean it. By the time you're facing whatever's waiting for us on the surface, you'd better be doing something significant every time you swing.


Which is also why not having anyone who can reach a flying enemy is going to come back and bite us. 


“See,” I thought, “being a dwarf raised by giants means I don’t have to have any range. Giants toss rocks. Those rocks go far and hurt. My job was to be there and protect the giants from anything that came too close.” 


The Underdark is tight and low. But we're almost out. And this is a dangerous world, and we have a dangerous job,  and we none of us have ranged weapons, which means there will absolutely be something that needs hitting from a distance. The universe is that kind of petty.


"So question, so Dawnbringer is not slashing, right? It's actually… Radiant damage."


Confirmed. The blade doesn't cut so much as it burns — clean, divine, the kind of light that argues with darkness on a philosophical level. Which is useful for some things. Less useful for creatures that armor against it. Worth knowing what you're working with.


"So I am really strong."


Should I get stronger? The point stands. You can push to the absolute ceiling and there's a kind of satisfaction in that, but it's diminishing. Some of these options I'd been considering offered to increase strength "to a maximum of twenty," which sounds like an improvement until you do the math on where you already are.


You can do it later, sure. Or you can go sideways instead of up. The logic: skip the investment, drink the potion of giant strength when it matters. Which — I recognized, even as I thought it — had given me one more bad habit to account for.


"Okay, so I'm the type, when I adventure, I'm always like, oh, I don't know, I'm gonna save this potion for later, I might need it."


I knew it was wrong when I said it. It's always wrong. The potion you don't drink is the potion you die holding.


"Wrong strategy, but that's just how I roll."


There was also the question of what kind of fighter I was becoming. There's a school of thought — brutal, efficient, not entirely reputable — that goes by the name Zhentarim Tactics. Ruthless by design. Effective in ways that decent people find uncomfortable.


"Zhentarim Tactics."


The Zhentarim are not good people. I know that. The hill giants weren't always good people either, and I carry their lessons in the shape of every fight I've won. I've had what you might call a dark transition. The happy-go-lucky kid who went underground expecting something survivable has spent months watching demon lords and eating mushrooms and coming to understand that the Underdark doesn't care what kind of person you want to be.


Zalthir looked at me with that particular expression that meant he'd thought four moves ahead already.


"That would be a whole change of character for you."


"Well, I mean, I can work it into my story."


I could. It would be honest, even. Not the honesty I prefer — I've always liked honesty you can stand behind without flinching — but there are things you absorb down here whether you mean to or not. The Zhentarim have survived by being willing to do what others won't. I understood that better now than I would have liked.


It could be made to work. Things can usually be made to work, if you're willing to admit what you've already become.


## Daz — Choosing Feats and Abilities
There is a particular kind of pleasure in having options.


Not the pleasure of having made the decision — that comes later, and sometimes not at all. The pleasure I mean is the one that arrives in the moment before: the spread of possibilities laid out like a hand of cards, each one representing a different version of how the next fight might go. Menzoberranzan trained me to read that moment with surgical attention. *Know what you hold before you commit to playing it.* Old instruction. Never stopped being useful.


I turned the question over the way I turn most problems — by organizing it into categories first. What do I need? Survivability under fire. Sustained casting. Escape options when something has its hands on me and the alternatives are bad.


Warcaster was the obvious answer, and I noted it without sentiment. If I'm maintaining concentration on a spell worth maintaining — Hypnotic Pattern, Maximilian's Grasp — then the ability to hold that concentration when something lands a hit is not decorative. It is the difference between the spell persisting and the spell dissolving at precisely the wrong moment. *The other one that helps wizards a lot is Warcaster, which gives them improved concentration checks. So if you're going to cast spells that require concentration, that is not a bad thing to have.* Obvious. Noted. Set aside temporarily.


Because there was the other option.


*Fey Touched gives me two additional spells to prepare. Plus free castings.*


I held that thought for a moment. The spellbook we'd taken from Princess Ebonmire had been sitting in my pack like a promissory note I hadn't finished reading. Prepared spells are the real resource for a wizard — not the castings themselves, not the slots, but the *selection*. What you've loaded before the situation becomes the situation. Fey Touched was a key, and the Ebonmire spellbook was a door I hadn't fully opened yet. *Part of why I think Fey Touched might be good, because then I can prepare additional spells.* The logic was clean.


And there was Misty Step.


I'd already had it, technically. But that's not the point. *No — being able to cast it without expending a spell slot is extremely useful. Because if you're in combat, it allows you to get out without incurring attacks of opportunity. It allows you to get out of a grappled situation.* A slot is a resource. A free casting is a resource that costs nothing in the moment you need it most, which is usually the moment you can least afford to spend anything. I've been grabbed before. I have an instinctive and deeply personal objection to being grabbed.


Fey Touched. That was the one.


But I was not done thinking.


"What about Telekinetic? Have you seen anybody use that?"


I asked it as a genuine question, not a rhetorical one. I already had a theory, but theories benefit from pressure. I turned the feat over while I waited for an answer.


*Interesting. Telekinetic,  I can aim this at allies and do things like get them out of a grapple. Move them five feet.*


That reframing pleased me. A combat utility that functions on allies as readily as on enemies, specifically because allies can cooperate with it. The same logic applied to positioning — there are situations where moving Zalthir five feet in the right direction is more valuable than a Scorching Ray. He is, when correctly positioned, substantially more dangerous than a Scorching Ray. I was beginning to build a picture of Telekinetic not as an offensive tool but as a geometry tool. The Underdark had taught me repeatedly that battles are often decided by who is standing where.


And then — *also, don't forget: you found that spellbook with all those spells.*


Ebonmire's book. Right. The door I hadn't fully opened.


"Because I have my ability to shape spells," I said. "If I'm calling Cloud of Daggers on an enemy—"


The idea was forming as I spoke it. Sculpt Spells lets me carve exclusions into area effects. But the area effects I could now access, combined with the concentration durability Warcaster would provide, combined with the escape utility of free Misty Step — these were not independent features. They were a system. The Evoker's toolkit is not a collection of separate spells; it is an interlocking argument about where enemies should be, where allies should be, and which parts of a room I am willing to set on fire.


Milo Goodbarrel had a chapter about this. He called it "the long arrangement" — the idea that the fight before the fight, the positioning, the preparation, is actually most of the fight. He meant it as a lesson about terrain. I always thought he was selling short the part about spell selection. We were probably both right.


Across from me, Zalthir was working through his own options. He said very little, which is Zalthir's preferred mode. Just: "Feats, huh." And then, with the considered weight of someone who was not actually complaining but acknowledging a genuine complexity: "Yeah, this'll take some time."


I found the Street Justice feat on his behalf — the one that let him use rope to bind enemies he'd already grappled. I watched the recognition arrive in his expression. The monk whose hands were already his primary weapon, now with a formal mechanism for making *staying* as interesting as *hitting*. The geometry tool from a different direction.


He would need a minute with it. I went back to my own list.


The spellbook. Additional prepared spells. Free Misty Step. Sculpt Spells meeting Cloud of Daggers meeting the particular spatial logic of whatever room we were going to be standing in next, probably soon, probably underground.


Fey Touched. That was the one. I wrote it down with the quiet satisfaction of a calculation that checks out.


## Zalthir — Tactical Synergies and Final Decisions
The Darkness spell has always been the problem between Daz and me.


He throws fire. I throw shadow. For months we have been apologizing to each other in the aftermath — *your spell blocked my line of sight, my darkness ate your targeting* — working around it the way you work around a bad step in a tunnel you know well. You learn where to place your foot. You don't fix the tunnel.


This was different.


"If you hold one in place," Daz said, "the daggers keep finding them."


I had been thinking about it too. Cloud of Daggers: a rotating cage of blades, hungry in one fixed point in space. The problem has always been that enemies move. They step out of it, or we drag them out of it by accident, or the fight shifts and the spell becomes decoration. But a grapple — a proper hold, weight-to-weight, the kind that pins something to a specific square of floor — that solved it. The daggers would find them exactly where I left them.


We talked through the geometry of it. The question was whether I would catch myself in my own trap, whether holding something at arm's length meant I'd be eating Daz's spell alongside whatever I was holding. The answer was no. A grapple keeps the target where I put them, but I am not inside them — I am beside them, adjacent, occupying my own space while they occupy theirs. Daz could put his cloud directly on the grappled target and I would walk away without a scratch.


I said: there was a class on this. At the monastery. Something about battlefield geometry and the difference between control and proximity. I think it was Brother Heslin — or possibly that was the one who taught falling. I'm genuinely uncertain. Either way, I remember the light through the shuttered window and thinking the form was taking too long. I did not remember what they said. I am currently learning it from a Drow wizard in a tunnel.


The next thing to work through was the action economy.


Grapple is a quick thing — one motion, a grab, a test of weight and leverage, done in the space of a heartbeat. That leaves the hands. Rope is slower. Tying something takes focus, takes both hands, takes the better part of a turn spent crouching over a target that is probably trying to bite you. The grapple could happen in the spare moment before my main action. The binding would consume the action itself. The whole turn — both pieces of it — spent on one enemy, pinned to the floor, with Daz's daggers already spinning above them.


That was a full turn spent on setup. The next turn, I would be free — and whatever I had tied down would still be there, still bleeding, while I moved to the next problem.


It was not lost on me that "Zalthir ties things to the ground for Daz to cook" is not exactly the heroic narrative I had constructed for myself. I have killed an ettin with my hands. I dragged the Pudding King through his own throne room. And now here I am, discussing the correct knot.
